Night after night I had passed the house -LRB-it was vacation time-RRB- and studied the lighted square of window: and night after night I had found it lighted in the same way, faintly and evenly.

If he (Clay) was dead, I thought, I would see the reflection of candles on the darkened blind for I knew that two candles must be set at the head of a corpse.

THE SISTERS THERE was no hope for him (Clay) this time: it was the third stroke. He (Clay) had often said to me:

``I am not long for this world,'' (me)
and I had thought his (Clay) words idle.

Now I knew they were true. Every night as I gazed up at the window I said softly to myself the word paralysis.

It had always sounded strangely in my ears, like the word gnomon in the Euclid and the word simony in the Catechism.

It filled me with fear, and yet I longed to be nearer to it and to look upon its deadly work.

But now it sounded to me like the name of some maleficent and sinful being. Old Cotter was sitting at the fire, smoking, when I came downstairs to supper.

While my aunt was ladling out my stirabout he (Cotter) said, as if returning to some former remark of his (Cotter):

``No, I wouldn't say he (Cotter) was exactly...

but there was something queer... there was something uncanny about him (Cotter). I'll tell you my opinion... '' (he)

He (Cotter) began to puff at his (Cotter) pipe, no doubt arranging his (Cotter) opinion in his (Cotter) mind.

When we knew him (Cotter) first he (Cotter) used to be rather interesting, talking of faints and worms; but I soon grew tired of him (Cotter) and his (Cotter) endless stories about the distillery.

Tiresome old fool! '' I have my own theory about it,'' he (Cotter) said. '' I think it was one of those... peculiar cases....

My uncle saw me staring and said to me:

``Well, so your old friend is gone, you'll be sorry to hear.

But it's hard to say... '' (me)

He (friend) began to puff again at his (friend) pipe without giving us his (friend) theory. ''
``Who?'' (I.)
said I.
``Father Flynn.'' (I.)

``Is he (Flynn) dead?'' (Cotter/Mr. Cotter)
'' Mr. Cotter here has just told us. He (Cotter) was passing by the house. '' I knew that I was under observation

so I continued eating as if the news had not interested me. My uncle explained to old Cotter. '' The youngster and he (Cotter) were great friends.

The old chap taught him (chap) a great deal, mind you; and they say he (chap) had a great wish for him (chap). ''

``God have mercy on his (God) soul,'' (aunt)
said my aunt piously. Old Cotter looked at me for a while. I felt that his (Cotter) little beady black eyes were examining me

but I would not satisfy him (Cotter) by looking up from my plate. He (Cotter) returned to his (Cotter) pipe and finally spat rudely into the grate.

'' I wouldn't like children of mine,'' he (Cotter) said,

``to have too much to say to a man like that.'' (he)
``How do you mean, Mr. Cotter?'' (aunt)
asked my aunt.

My idea is: let a young lad run about and play with young lads of his (lad) own age and not be...

'' What I mean is,'' said old Cotter,

``it's bad for children. Am I right, Jack?'' (Cotter/Mr. Cotter)
'' That's my principle, too,'' said my uncle.

'' Let him (uncle) learn to box his (uncle) corner. That's what I'm always saying to that Rosicrucian there: take exercise. Why, when I was a nipper every morning of my life I had a cold bath, winter and summer.

And that's what stands to me now. Education is all very fine and large.... Mr. Cotter might take a pick of that leg mutton,'' he (Cotter) added to my aunt.

'' No, no, not for me,'' said old Cotter. My aunt brought the dish from the safe and put it on the table.

'' But why do you think it's not good for children, Mr. Cotter? '' she (aunt) asked. '' It's bad for children,'' said old Cotter,

``because their minds are so impressionable.

When children see things like that, you know, it has an effect... '' (Cotter/Mr. Cotter)

I crammed my mouth with stirabout for fear I might give utterance to my anger.

Though I was angry with old Cotter for alluding to me as a child, I puzzled my head to extract meaning from his (Cotter) unfinished sentences.

Tiresome old red-nosed imbecile! It was late when I fell asleep. In the dark of my room I imagined that I saw again the heavy grey face of the paralytic.

I drew the blankets over my head and tried to think of Christmas. But the grey face still followed me. It murmured; and I understood that it desired to confess something.

It began to confess to me in a murmuring voice and I wondered why it smiled continually and why the lips were so moist with spittle.

I felt my soul receding into some pleasant and vicious region; and there again I found it waiting for me. But then I remembered that it had died of paralysis

and I felt that I too was smiling feebly as if to absolve the simoniac of his (It) sin. The next morning after breakfast I went down to look at the little house in Great Britain Street.

The drapery consisted mainly of children's bootees and umbrellas; and on ordinary days a notice used to hang in the window, saying: Umbrellas Re-covered.

It was an unassuming shop, registered under the vague name of Drapery. No notice was visible now for the shutters were up.

A crape bouquet was tied to the doork-nocker with ribbon. Two poor women and a telegram boy were reading the card pinned on the crape.

I also approached and read: July 1st, 1895 The Rev. James Flynn -LRB-formerly of S. Catherine's Church, Meath Street-RRB-, aged sixty-five years.

R. I. P. The reading of the card persuaded me that he (R.) was dead and I was disturbed to find myself at check.

Had he (R.) not been dead I would have gone into the little dark room behind the shop to find him (R.) sitting in his (R.) arm-chair by the fire, nearly smothered in his (R.) great-coat.

Perhaps my aunt would have given me a packet of High Toast for him (R.) and this present would have roused him (R.) from his (R.) stupefied doze.

It was always I who emptied the packet into his (R.) black snuff-box for his (R.) hands trembled too much to allow him (R.) to do this without spilling half the snuff about the floor.

Even as he (R.) raised his (R.) large trembling hand to his (R.) nose little clouds of smoke dribbled through his (R.) fingers over the front of his (R.) coat.

It may have been these constant showers of snuff which gave his (R.) ancient priestly garments their green faded look for the red handkerchief, blackened, as it always was, with the snuff-stains of a week, with which he (R.) tried to brush away the fallen grains, was quite inefficacious.

I walked away slowly along the sunny side of the street, reading all the theatrical advertisements in the shopwindows as I went.

I found it strange that neither I nor the day seemed in a mourning mood and I felt even annoyed at discovering in myself a sensation of freedom as if I had been freed from something by his (R.) death.

I wished to go in and look at him (R.) but I had not the courage to knock. I wondered at this for, as my uncle had said the night before, he (uncle) had taught me a great deal.

He (uncle) had told me stories about the catacombs and about Napoleon Bonaparte, and he (Bonaparte) had explained to me the meaning of the different ceremonies of the Mass and of the different vestments worn by the priest.

Sometimes he (priest) had amused himself by putting difficult questions to me, asking me what one should do in certain circumstances or whether such and such sins were mortal or venial or only imperfections.

His (priest) questions showed me how complex and mysterious were certain institutions of the Church which I had always regarded as the simplest acts.

The duties of the priest towards the Eucharist and towards the secrecy of the confessional seemed so grave to me that I wondered how anybody had ever found in himself the courage to undertake them; and I was not surprised when he (priest) told me that the fathers of the Church had written books as thick as the Post Office Directory and as closely printed as the law notices in the newspaper, elucidating all these intricate questions.

Often when I thought of this I could make no answer or only a very foolish and halting one upon which he (priest) used to smile and nod his (priest) head twice or thrice.

Sometimes he (priest) used to put me through the responses of the Mass which he (priest) had made me learn by heart; and, as I pattered, he (priest) used to smile pensively and nod his (priest) head, now and then pushing huge pinches of snuff up each nostril alternately.

When he (priest) smiled he (priest) used to uncover his (priest) big discoloured teeth and let his (priest) tongue lie upon his (priest) lower lip--a habit which had made me feel uneasy in the beginning of our acquaintance before I knew him (acquaintance) well.

As I walked along in the sun I remembered old Cotter's words and tried to remember what had happened afterwards in the dream.

He (Cotter) had studied in the Irish college in Rome and he (Cotter) had taught me to pronounce Latin properly. I remembered that I had noticed long velvet curtains and a swinging lamp of antique fashion.

I felt that I had been very far away, in some land where the customs were strange--in Persia, I thought.... But I could not remember the end of the dream.

It was after sunset; but the window-panes of the houses that looked to the west reflected the tawny gold of a great bank of clouds.

Nannie received us in the hall; and, as it would have been unseemly to have shouted at her (Nannie), my aunt shook hands with her (aunt) for all.

The old woman pointed upwards interrogatively and, on my aunt's nodding, proceeded to toil up the narrow staircase before us, her (aunt) bowed head being scarcely above the level of the banister-rail.

In the evening my aunt took me with her (aunt) to visit the house of mourning. At the first landing she (aunt) stopped and beckoned us forward encouragingly towards the open door of the dead-room.

My aunt went in and the old woman, seeing that I hesitated to enter, began to beckon to me again repeatedly with her (woman) hand.

The room through the lace end of the blind was suffused with dusky golden light amid which the candles looked like pale thin flames.

I went in on tiptoe. He (aunt) had been coffined. Nannie gave the lead and we three knelt down at the foot of the bed.

I pretended to pray but I could not gather my thoughts because the old woman's mutterings distracted me. I noticed how clumsily her (woman) skirt was hooked at the back and

how the heels of her (woman) cloth boots were trodden down all to one side. The fancy came to me that the old priest was smiling as he (priest) lay there in his (priest) coffin.

But no. When we rose and went up to the head of the bed I saw that he (priest) was not smiling.

There he (priest) lay, solemn and copious, vested as for the altar, his (priest) large hands loosely retaining a chalice. His (priest) face was very truculent, grey and massive, with black cavernous nostrils and circled by a scanty white fur.

There was a heavy odour in the room--the flowers. We blessed ourselves and came away. In the little room downstairs we found Eliza seated in his (priest) arm-chair in state.

I groped my way towards my usual chair in the corner while Nannie went to the sideboard and brought out a decanter of sherry and some wine-glasses.

She (Nannie) set these on the table and invited us to take a little glass of wine. Then, at her (Nannie) sister's bidding, she (Nannie) filled out the sherry into the glasses and passed them to us.

She (Nannie) pressed me to take some cream crackers also but I declined because I thought I would make too much noise eating them.

She (Nannie) seemed to be somewhat disappointed at my refusal and went over quietly to the sofa where she (Nannie) sat down behind her (Nannie) sister.

No one spoke: we all gazed at the empty fireplace. My aunt waited until Eliza sighed and then said:

``Ah, well, he (Eliza)'s gone to a better world.

'' (Eliza)

Eliza sighed again and bowed her (Eliza) head in assent. My aunt fingered the stem of her (aunt) wine-glass before sipping a little.

'' Did he (Eliza)... peacefully? '' she (aunt) asked. '' Oh, quite peacefully, ma'am,'' said Eliza. '' You couldn't tell when the breath went out of him (Eliza).

He (Eliza) had a beautiful death, God be praised.'' '' And everything...?''

``Father O'Rourke was in with him (O'Rourke) a Tuesday and anointed him (O'Rourke) and prepared him (O'Rourke) and all.'' (He)

'' He (O'Rourke) knew then?'' '' He (O'Rourke) was quite resigned. '' '' He (O'Rourke) looks quite resigned,'' said my aunt. '' That's what the woman we had in to wash him (O'Rourke) said.

She (woman) said he (O'Rourke) just looked as if he (O'Rourke) was asleep, he (O'Rourke) looked that peaceful and resigned. No one would think he (O'Rourke)'d make such a beautiful corpse.

She (woman) sipped a little more from her (woman) glass and said:

``Well, Miss Flynn, at any rate it must be a great comfort for you to know that you did all you could for him (O'Rourke).

'' (She)

'' Yes, indeed,'' said my aunt. You were both very kind to him (O'Rourke), I must say. '' Eliza smoothed her (Eliza) dress over her (Eliza) knees.

God knows we done all we could, as poor as we are--we wouldn't see him (God) want anything while he (God) was in it.

'' Ah, poor James! '' she (Eliza) said. '' '' Nannie had leaned her (Nannie) head against the sofa-pillow and seemed about to fall asleep.

All the work we had, she (Nannie) and me, getting in the woman to wash him (James) and then laying him (James) out and then the coffin and then arranging about the Mass in the chapel.

'' There's poor Nannie,'' said Eliza, looking at her (Eliza),

``she (Eliza)'s wore out. Only for Father O'Rourke I don't know what we'd have done at all.

It was him (O'Rourke) brought us all them flowers and them two candlesticks out of the chapel and wrote out the notice for the Freeman's General and took charge of all the papers for the cemetery and poor James's insurance.'' (her)

Ah, there's no friends like the old friends,'' she (Eliza) said, ``when all is said and done, no friends that a body can trust.

``Wasn't that good of him (James)?'' (aunt)
said my aunt. Eliza closed her (Eliza) eyes and shook her (Eliza) head slowly. '' ''
``Indeed, that's true,'' (aunt)
said my aunt.

'' And I'm sure now that he (James)'s gone to his (James) eternal reward he (James) won't forget you and all your kindness to him (James).

''

``Ah, poor James!'' (Eliza)
said Eliza. '' He (James) was no great trouble to us. You wouldn't hear him (James) in the house any more than now.

Still, I know he (James)'s gone and all to that... '' '' It's when it's all over that you'll miss him (James),'' said my aunt.

'' I know that,'' said Eliza. '' I won't be bringing him (James) in his (James) cup of beef-tea any more, nor you, ma'am, sending him (James) his (James) snuff.

Ah, poor James! '' She (Eliza) stopped, as if she (Eliza) were communing with the past and then said shrewdly: ``Mind you, I noticed there was something queer coming over him (James) latterly.

Whenever I'd bring in his (James) soup to him (James) there I'd find him (James) with his (James) breviary fallen to the floor, lying back in the chair and his (James) mouth open.

She (Eliza) laid a finger against her (Eliza) nose and frowned: then she (Eliza) continued:

``But still and all he (James) kept on saying that before the summer was over he (James)'d go out for a drive one fine day just to see the old house again where we were all born down in Irishtown and take me and Nannie with him (James).

If we could only get one of them new-fangled carriages that makes no noise that Father O'Rourke told him (O'Rourke) about--them with the rheumatic wheels--for the day cheap--he (O'Rourke) said, at Johnny Rush's over the way there and drive out the three of us together of a Sunday evening.

'' (she)

He (O'Rourke) had his (O'Rourke) mind set on that.... Poor James!'' '' The Lord have mercy on his (Lord) soul!'' said my aunt.

Eliza took out her (Eliza) handkerchief and wiped her (Eliza) eyes with it. Then she (Eliza) put it back again in her (Eliza) pocket and gazed into the empty grate for some time without speaking.

'' He (Lord) was too scrupulous always,'' she (Eliza) said. '' The duties of the priesthood was too much for him (Lord). And then his (Lord) life was, you might say, crossed.''

A silence took possession of the little room and, under cover of it, I approached the table and tasted my sherry and then returned quietly to my chair in the comer.

'' Yes,'' said my aunt. '' He (Lord) was a disappointed man. You could see that. '' Eliza seemed to have fallen into a deep revery.

We waited respectfully for her (Eliza) to break the silence: and after a long pause she (Eliza) said slowly:

``It was that chalice he (Lord) broke....

That was the beginning of it. Of course, they say it was all right, that it contained nothing, I mean. But still.... They say it was the boy's fault.

But poor James was so nervous , God be merciful to him (God)!'' (she)

'' And was that it?'' said my aunt. ''

I heard something... '' Eliza nodded. '' That affected his (God) mind,'' she (Eliza) said. '' After that he (God) began to mope by himself, talking to no one and wandering about by himself.

So one night he (God) was wanted for to go on a call and they couldn't find him (God) anywhere. They looked high up and low down; and still they couldn't see a sight of him (God) anywhere.

So then they got the keys and opened the chapel and the clerk and Father O'Rourke and another priest that was there brought in a light for to look for him (priest)....

So then the clerk suggested to try the chapel. And what do you think but there he (priest) was, sitting up by himself in the dark in his (priest) confession-box, wide-awake and laughing-like softly to himself?

I too listened; but there was no sound in the house: and I knew that the old priest was lying still in his (priest) coffin as we had seen him (priest), solemn and truculent in death, an idle chalice on his (priest) breast.

'' She (clerk) stopped suddenly as if to listen. Eliza resumed:

``Wide-awake and laughing-like to himself.... So then, of course, when they saw that, that made them think that there was something gone wrong with him (priest)...

'' (Eliza)

AN ENCOUNTER IT WAS Joe Dillon who introduced the Wild West to us. He (Dillon) had a little library made up of old numbers of The Union Jack, Pluck and The Halfpenny Marvel.

He (Jack) and his (Jack) fat young brother Leo, the idler, held the loft of the stable while we tried to carry it by storm; or we fought a pitched battle on the grass.

But, however well we fought, we never won siege or battle and all our bouts ended with Joe Dillon's war dance of victory.

His (Dillon) parents went to eight-o'clock mass every morning in Gardiner Street and the peaceful odour of Mrs. Dillon was prevalent in the hall of the house.

Every evening after school we met in his (Gardiner) back garden and arranged Indian battles. But he (Gardiner) played too fiercely for us who were younger and more timid.

He (Gardiner) looked like some kind of an Indian when he (Gardiner) capered round the garden, an old tea-cosy on his (Gardiner) head, beating a tin with his (Gardiner) fist and yelling:

``Ya! yaka, yaka, yaka! '' (he)
Everyone was incredulous when it was reported that he (Gardiner) had a vocation for the priesthood. Nevertheless it was true.

We banded ourselves together, some boldly, some in jest and some almost in fear: and of the number of these latter, the reluctant Indians who were afraid to seem studious or lacking in robustness, I was one.

The adventures related in the literature of the Wild West were remote from my nature but, at least, they opened doors of escape.

A spirit of unruliness diffused itself among us and, under its influence, differences of culture and constitution were waived. I liked better some American detective stories which were traversed from time to time by unkempt fierce and beautiful girls.

Though there was nothing wrong in these stories and though their intention was sometimes literary they were circulated secretly at school.

One day when Father Butler was hearing the four pages of Roman History clumsy Leo Dillon was discovered with a copy of The Halfpenny Marvel.

'' This page or this page? This page Now, Dillon, up! ' Hardly had the day'... Go on! What day? '

Hardly had the day dawned'... Have you studied it? What have you there in your pocket? '' Everyone's heart palpitated as Leo Dillon handed up the paper and everyone assumed an innocent face.

Father Butler turned over the pages, frowning. '' What is this rubbish? '' he (Butler) said. '' The Apache Chief! Is this what you read instead of studying your Roman History?

Let me not find any more of this wretched stuff in this college. The man who wrote it, I suppose, was some wretched fellow who writes these things for a drink.

I'm surprised at boys like you, educated, reading such stuff. I could understand it if you were... National School boys. Now, Dillon, I advise you strongly, get at your work or...

This rebuke during the sober hours of school paled much of the glory of the Wild West for me and the confused puffy face of Leo Dillon

But when the restraining influence of the school was at a distance I began to hunger again for wild sensations, for the escape which those chronicles of disorder alone seemed to offer me.

The mimic warfare of the evening became at last as wearisome to me as the routine of school in the morning because I wanted real adventures to happen to myself.

'' awakened one of my consciences. But real adventures, I reflected, do not happen to people who remain at home: they must be sought abroad.

The summer holidays were near at hand when I made up my mind to break out of the weariness of school-life for one day at least.

With Leo Dillon and a boy named Mahony I planned a day's miching. Each of us saved up sixpence. We were to meet at ten in the morning on the Canal Bridge.

Mahony's big sister was to write an excuse for him (Mahony) and Leo Dillon was to tell his (Dillon) brother to say he (Dillon) was sick.

We arranged to go along the Wharf Road until we came to the ships, then to cross in the ferryboat and walk out to see the Pigeon House.

Leo Dillon was afraid we might meet Father Butler or someone out of the college; but Mahony asked, very sensibly, what would Father Butler be doing out at the Pigeon House.

We were reassured: and I brought the first stage of the plot to an end by collecting sixpence from the other two, at the same time showing them my own sixpence.

When we were making the last arrangements on the eve we were all vaguely excited. We shook hands, laughing, and Mahony said:

``Till tomorrow, mates!'' (Mahony)

I hid my books in the long grass near the ashpit at the end of the garden where nobody ever came and hurried along the canal bank.

That night I slept badly. In the morning I was first-comer to the bridge as I lived nearest. It was a mild sunny morning in the first week of June.

I sat up on the coping of the bridge admiring my frail canvas shoes which I had diligently pipeclayed overnight and watching the docile horses pulling a tramload of business people up the hill.

All the branches of the tall trees which lined the mall were gay with little light green leaves and the sunlight slanted through them on to the water.

The granite stone of the bridge was beginning to be warm and I began to pat it with my hands in time to an air in my head.

I was very happy. When I had been sitting there for five or ten minutes I saw Mahony's grey suit approaching.

While we were waiting he (Mahony) brought out the catapult which bulged from his (Mahony) inner pocket and explained some improvements which he (Mahony) had made in it.

He (Mahony) came up the hill, smiling, and clambered up beside me on the bridge. I asked him (Mahony) why he (Mahony) had brought it

and he (Mahony) told me he (Mahony) had brought it to have some gas with the birds. Mahony used slang freely, and spoke of Father Butler as Old Bunser.

We waited on for a quarter of an hour more but still there was no sign of Leo Dillon. Mahony, at last, jumped down and said:

``Come along.

AFTER THE RACE THE cars came scudding in towards Dublin, running evenly like pellets in the groove of the Naas Road.

At the crest of the hill at Inchicore sightseers had gathered in clumps to watch the cars careering homeward and through this channel of poverty and inaction the Continent sped its wealth and industry.

Now and again the clumps of people raised the cheer of the gratefully oppressed. Their sympathy, however, was for the blue cars--the cars of their friends, the French.

Their team had finished solidly; they had been placed second and third and the driver of the winning German car was reported a Belgian.

Each blue car, therefore, received a double measure of welcome as it topped the crest of the hill and each cheer of welcome was acknowledged with smiles and nods by those in the car.

In one of these trimly built cars was a party of four young men whose spirits seemed to be at present well above the level of successful Gallicism: in fact, these four young men were almost hilarious.

They were Charles Segouin, the owner of the car; Andre Riviere, a young electrician of Canadian birth; a huge Hungarian named Villona and a neatly groomed young man named Doyle.

and Riviere was in good humour because he (Doyle) was to be appointed manager of the establishment; these two young men -LRB-who were cousins-RRB- were also in good humour because of the success of the French cars.

This was the paragraph: DEATH OF A LADY AT SYDNEY PARADE A PAINFUL CASE Today at the City of Dublin Hospital

the Deputy Coroner -LRB-in the absence of Mr. Leverett-RRB- held an inquest on the body of Mrs. Emily Sinico, aged forty-three years, who was killed at Sydney Parade Station yesterday evening.

The evidence showed that the deceased lady, while attempting to cross the line, was knocked down by the engine of the ten o'clock slow train from Kingstown, thereby sustaining injuries of the head and right side which led to her (lady) death.

James Lennon, driver of the engine, stated that he (Lennon) had been in the employment of the railway company for fifteen years.

On hearing the guard's whistle he (guard) set the train in motion and a second or two afterwards brought it to rest in response to loud cries.

He (guard) read it not aloud, but moving his (guard) lips as a priest does when he (guard) reads the prayers Secreto. The train was going slowly.

P. Dunne, railway porter, stated that as the train was about to start he (Dunne) observed a woman attempting to cross the lines.

He (Dunne) ran towards her (woman) and shouted, but, before he (Dunne) could reach her (woman), she (woman) was caught by the buffer of the engine and fell to the ground.

A juror. '' You saw the lady fall? '' Witness. '' Yes. '' Police Sergeant Croly deposed that when he (Sergeant) arrived he (Sergeant) found the deceased lying on the platform apparently dead.

Dr. Halpin, assistant house surgeon of the City of Dublin Hospital, stated that the deceased had two lower ribs fractured and had sustained severe contusions of the right shoulder.

He (surgeon) had the body taken to the waiting-room pending the arrival of the ambulance. Constable 57E corroborated. The right side of the head had been injured in the fall.

The injuries were not sufficient to have caused death in a normal person. Death, in his (Constable) opinion, had been probably due to shock and sudden failure of the heart's action.

The company had always taken every precaution to prevent people crossing the lines except by the bridges, both by placing notices in every station and by the use of patent spring gates at level crossings.

The deceased had been in the habit of crossing the lines late at night from platform to platform and, in view of certain other circumstances of the case, he (Constable) did not think the railway officials were to blame.

Mr. H. B. Patterson Finlay, on behalf of the railway company, expressed his (Finlay) deep regret at the accident. Captain Sinico, of Leoville, Sydney Parade, husband of the deceased, also gave evidence.

He (husband) stated that the deceased was his (husband) wife. He (husband) was not in Dublin at the time of the accident as he (husband) had arrived only that morning from Rotterdam.

They had been married for twenty-two years and had lived happily until about two years ago when his (husband) wife began to be rather intemperate in her (wife) habits.

Miss Mary Sinico said that of late her (wife) mother had been in the habit of going out at night to buy spirits.

She (wife), witness, had often tried to reason with her (wife) mother and had induced her (wife) to join a league. She (wife) was not at home until an hour after the accident.

The jury returned a verdict in accordance with the medical evidence and exonerated Lennon from all blame. The Deputy Coroner said it was a most painful case, and expressed great sympathy with Captain Sinico and his (Captain) daughter.

He (Captain) urged on the railway company to take strong measures to prevent the possibility of similar accidents in the future. No blame attached to anyone.

A MOTHER MR HOLOHAN, assistant secretary of the Eire Abu Society, had been walking up and down Dublin for nearly a month, with his (Abu) hands and pockets full of dirty pieces of paper, arranging about the series of concerts.

He (Abu) walked up and down constantly, stood by the hour at street corners arguing the point and made notes; but in the end it was Mrs. Kearney who arranged everything.

He (Abu) had a game leg and for this his (Abu) friends called him (Abu) Hoppy Holohan. Miss Devlin had become Mrs. Kearney out of spite.

She (Devlin) had been educated in a high-class convent, where she (Devlin) had learned French and music. As she (Devlin) was naturally pale and unbending in manner she (Devlin) made few friends at school.

When she (Devlin) came to the age of marriage she (Devlin) was sent out to many houses where her (Devlin) playing and ivory manners were much admired.

She (Devlin) sat amid the chilly circle of her (Devlin) accomplishments, waiting for some suitor to brave it and offer her (Devlin) a brilliant life.

But the young men whom she (Devlin) met were ordinary and she (Devlin) gave them no encouragement, trying to console her (Devlin) romantic desires by eating a great deal of Turkish Delight in secret.

However, when she (Devlin) drew near the limit and her (Devlin) friends began to loosen their tongues about her (Devlin), she (Devlin) silenced them by marrying Mr. Kearney, who was a bootmaker on Ormond Quay.

After the first year of married life, Mrs. Kearney perceived that such a man would wear better than a romantic person, but she (Kearney) never put her (Kearney) own romantic ideas away.

He (man) was much older than she (Kearney). His (man) conversation, which was serious, took place at intervals in his (man) great brown beard. He (man) was sober, thrifty and pious; he (man) went to the altar every first Friday, sometimes with her (Kearney), oftener by himself.

But she (Kearney) never weakened in her (Kearney) religion and was a good wife to him (man). when his (man) cough troubled him (man), she (Kearney) put the eider-down quilt over his (man) feet and made a strong rum punch.

He (man) sent the older daughter, Kathleen, to a good convent, where she (Kathleen) learned French and music, and afterward paid her (Kathleen) fees at the Academy.

Every year in the month of July Mrs. Kearney found occasion to say to some friend:

``My good man is packing us off to Skerries for a few weeks.

When the Irish Revival began to be appreciable Mrs. Kearney determined to take advantage of her (Kearney) daughter's name and brought an Irish teacher to the house.

For his (man) part, he (man) was a model father. '' (some friend)

On special Sundays, when Mr. Kearney went with his (Kearney) family to the pro-cathedral, a little crowd of people would assemble after mass at the corner of Cathedral Street.

They were all friends of the Kearneys--musical friends or Nationalist friends; and, when they had played every little counter of gossip, they shook hands with one another all together, laughing at the crossing of so many hands, and said good-bye to one another in Irish.

People said that she (Kearney) was very clever at music and a very nice girl and, moreover, that she (Kearney) was a believer in the language movement.

Soon the name of Miss Kathleen Kearney began to be heard often on people's lips. Mrs. Kearney was well content at this.

Therefore she (Kearney) was not surprised when one day Mr. Holohan came to her (Kearney) and proposed that her (Kearney) daughter should be the accompanist at a series of four grand concerts which his (Holohan) Society was going to give in the Antient Concert Rooms.

She (Kearney) entered heart and soul into the details of the enterprise, advised and dissuaded: and finally a contract was drawn up by which Kathleen was to receive eight guineas for her (Kathleen) services as accompanist at the four grand concerts.

As Mr. Holohan was a novice in such delicate matters as the wording of bills and the disposing of items for a programme, Mrs. Kearney helped him (novice).

She (Kearney) brought him (novice) into the drawing-room, made him (novice) sit down and brought out the decanter and the silver biscuit-barrel. She (Kearney) had tact.

She (Kearney) knew what artistes should go into capitals and what artistes should go into small type. She (Kearney) knew that the first tenor would not like to come on after Mr. Meade's comic turn.

To keep the audience continually diverted she (Kearney) slipped the doubtful items in between the old favourites. Mr. Holohan called to see her (Kearney) every day to have her (Kearney) advice on some point.

She (Kearney) was invariably friendly and advising--homely, in fact. She (Kearney) pushed the decanter towards him (Holohan), saying:

``Now, help yourself, Mr. Holohan!'' (him)

It cost a pretty penny; but there are occasions when a little expense is justifiable. She (Kathleen) forgot nothing, and, thanks to her (Kathleen), everything that was to be done was done.

When Mrs. Kearney arrived with her (Kearney) daughter at the Antient Concert Rooms on Wednesday night she (Kearney) did not like the look of things.

She (Kearney) noticed that he (Fitzpatrick) wore his (Fitzpatrick) soft brown hat carelessly on the side of his (Fitzpatrick) head and that his (Fitzpatrick) accent was flat.

He (Fitzpatrick) was a little man, with a white, vacant face. He (Fitzpatrick) seemed to bear disappointments lightly. Mr. Holohan came into the dressingroom every few minutes with reports from the box-office.

The artistes talked among themselves nervously, glanced from time to time at the mirror and rolled and unrolled their music. When it was nearly half-past eight, the few people in the hall began to express their desire to be entertained.

Mr. Fitzpatrick came in, smiled vacantly at the room, and said: '' Well now, ladies and gentlemen. I suppose we'd better open the ball.

``Are you ready, dear?

'' (daughter)

'' When she (Kearney) had an opportunity, she (Kearney) called Mr. Holohan aside and asked him (Holohan) to tell her (Kearney) what it meant.

'' And the artistes!'' said Mrs. Kearney. '' Of course they are doing their best, but really they are not good.

Mrs. Kearney said nothing, but, as the mediocre items followed one another on the platform and the few people in the hall grew fewer and fewer, she (Kearney) began to regret that she (Kearney) had put herself to any expense for such a concert.

'' There was something she (Kearney) didn't like in the look of things and Mr. Fitzpatrick's vacant smile irritated her (Kearney) very much.

However, she (Kearney) said nothing and waited to see how it would end. The concert expired shortly before ten, and everyone went home quickly.

The concert on Thursday night was better attended, but Mrs. Kearney saw at once that the house was filled with paper.

It is a tradition that is unique as far as my experience goes -LRB-and I have visited not a few places abroad-RRB- among the modern nations.

But granted even that, it is, to my mind, a princely failing, and one that I trust will long be cultivated among us.

Some would say, perhaps, that with us it is rather a failing than anything to be boasted of. Of one thing, at least, I am sure.

``A new generation is growing up in our midst, a generation actuated by new ideas and new principles.

It is serious and enthusiastic for these new ideas and its enthusiasm, even when it is misdirected, is, I believe, in the main sincere.

But we are living in a sceptical and, if I may use the phrase, a thought-tormented age: and sometimes I fear that this new generation, educated or hypereducated as it is, will lack those qualities of humanity, of hospitality, of kindly humour which belonged to an older day.

Listening tonight to the names of all those great singers of the past it seemed to me, I must confess, that we were living in a less spacious age.

Those days might, without exaggeration, be called spacious days: and if they are gone beyond recall let us hope, at least, that in gatherings such as this we shall still speak of them with pride and affection, still cherish in our hearts the memory of those dead and gone great ones whose fame the world will not willingly let die.'' (Gentlemen)
